FOR EDUCATIONAL USE ONLY

 Maneka Gandhi v Union of India

Supreme Court of India

25 January 1978

Case Analysis

Bench

P.N. Bhagwati, M. Hameedullah Beg, N.L. Untwalia, P.S. Kailasam, S. Murtaza Fazal Ali, V.R. Krishna Iyer, Y.V. Chandrachud

Where Reported

1978 Indlaw SC 212; (1978) 1 SCC 248; AIR 1978 SC 597; 1978 CRLJ 1797; 1978 (2) SCJ 312; [1978] 2 S.C.R. 621

Case Digest

Subject: Constitution; Defence & Security Forces

Keywords: Freedom Of Speech And Expression, Sovereignty And Integrity Of India, Competence Of The Legislature, Freedom Of Movement, Morality, Universal Declaration of Human Rights, Constitutionality, Working Journalists (Conditions of Service) and Miscellaneous Provisions Act, 1955, Banking Companies (Acquisition and Transfer of Undertaking) Act, 1969

Summary: (A) Constitution - Passport Act, 1967, s. 10(3)(c) - Constitution of India, 1950, arts. 14, 16 and 21 - Constitutionality of sub-section (3)(c) of s. 10 - Validity of order impounding passport - Petitioner's passport was impounded u/s. 10(3)(c) by Regional Passport Officer - Petitioner's request to furnish a copy of statement of reasons for making order as provided in s. 10(5) was refused on ground that Government decided 'in interest of general public' not to furnish her a copy of statement of reasons, for making of order - Present petition - (A) Opportunity to holder of passport to be heard in defence - Held, procedure 'established' by Passports Act, 1967 for impounding a passport is in conformity with requirement of Art. 21 and does not fall foul of that article - However, in present case, Central Government was unjustified in withholding reasons for impounding passport from petitioner and this was not only in breach of statutory provision, but it also amounted to denial of opportunity of hearing to petitioner - Order impounding passport of petitioner was, therefore, clearly in violation of rule of natural justice embodied in maxim audi alteram partem and it was not in conformity with procedure prescribed by Passports Act, 1967 - (B) Constitutional validity of impugned order - Held, if an order impounding a passport is made in interests of public order, decency or morality, restriction imposed by it may be so wide, excessive or disproportionate to mischief or evil sought to be averted that it may be considered unreasonable and in that event, if direct and inevitable consequence of Order is to abridge or take away freedom of speech and expression, it would be violative of Art. 19(1)(a) and would not be protected by Art. 19(2) and same would be position where the order is in interests of the general public but it impinges directly and inevitably on freedom to carry on a profession in which case it would contravene Art. 19(1)(g) without being saved by provision enacted in Art. 19(6) - Impugned Order in present case do not violate either Art. 19(1)(a) or Art. 19(1) (g) - Direct and inevitable consequence of impugned order was to impede exercise of her right to go abroad and not to interfere with her freedom of speech and expression or her right to carry on her profession - (C) Whether impugned Order is within authority conferred by s. 10(3)(c)? - Reasons for impugned order accordingly were that, according to Central Government, petitioner was involved in matters coming within purview of Commissions of Inquiry constituted by Government of India to inquire into excesses committed during emergency and in respect of matters concerning Maruti and its associate companies and Central Government was of view that petitioner should be available in India to give evidence before these Commissions of Inquiry and according to a report received by Central Government on that day, there was likelihood of her leaving India - Held, it is not for court to decide whether presence of petitioner is likely to be required for giving evidence before the Commissions of Inquiry - Government, which has instituted Commissions of Inquiry, would be best in a position to know, having regard to material before it, whether presence of petitioner is likely to be required - It may be that her presence may ultimately not be required at all, but at present stage, question is only whether her presence is likely to be required and so far that is concerned, view taken by Government cannot be regarded as so unreasonable or perverse - No reason to interfere with impugned Order made by Central Government - Writ petition disposed of.

(B) Constitution - Passport Act, 1967, s. 10(3)(c) - Constitution of India, 1950, arts. 14 - Whether s. 10(3)(c) violative of art. 14? - It was submitted that s. 10(3)(c) confers unguided and unfettered power on Passport Authority to impound a passport and hence it is violative of equality clause contained in art. 14 - There are four grounds set out in s. 10(3)(c) which would justify making of an order impounding a passport and last ground denoted by words 'in interests of general public' - Held, words 'in interests of general public' have a clearly well defined meaning - Sufficient guidelines are provided by the words 'in interests of general public' and power conferred on Passport Authority to impound a passport cannot be said to be unguided or unfettered - Moreover, power is exercised by Central Government itself and it can safely be assumed that Central Government will exercise power in a reasonable and responsible manner - Power conferred on Passport Authority to impound a passport u/s. 10(3)(c) cannot, therefore, be regarded as discriminatory and it does not fall foul of Art. 14 - Writ petition disposed of.

(C) Constitution - Passport Act, 1967, s. 10(3)(c) - Constitution of India, 1950, art. 21 - Meaning and content of personal liberty in art. 21 - Whether s. 10(3)(c) is ultra vires art. 21? - Held, expression 'personal liberty' in art. 21 is of widest amplitude and it covers a variety of rights which go to constitute personal liberty of man and some of them have been raised to status of distinct fundamental rights and given additional protection u/art. 19 - Thus, no person can be deprived of his right to go abroad unless there is a law made by State prescribing procedure for so depriving him and deprivation is effected strictly in accordance with such procedure - It was for this reason, in order to comply with requirement of art. 21, that Parliament enacted Passports Act, 1967 for regulating right to go abroad - It is clear from provisions of Passports, Act, 1967 that is lays down circumstances under which a passport may be issued or refused or cancelled or impounded and also prescribes a procedure for doing so - Writ petition disposed of.

(D) Constitution - Approach to be adopted by Court in adjudging constitutionality of a statute on touchstone of fundamental rights - Test to be applied for determining whether a statute infringes a particular fundamental right - Held, some concept-vehicle would be needed to quantify extent of directness or indirectness in order to apply test and that is supplied by criterion of 'inevitable' consequence or effect adumbrated in Express Newspaper's case 1958 INDLAW SC 3 - This criterion helps to quantify extent of directness necessary to constitute infringement of a fundamental right is direct and inevitable, then a fortiori it must be presumed to have been intended by authority taking action and hence this doctrine of direct and inevitable effect has been described by some jurists as doctrine of intended and real effect - This is test which must be applied for purpose of determining whether s. 10(3)(c) or impugned order made under it is violative of Art. 19(1)(a) or 19(1)(g) - Writ petition disposed of.

(E) Constitution - Passport Act, 1967, s. 10(3)(c) - Constitution of India, 1950, arts. 19(1)(a) and 19(1)(g) - Scope and ambit of right of free speech and expression conferred u/art. 19(1)(a) - Art. 19(1)(a) declare freedom of speech and expression as a fundamental right and to protect it against State action - (A) Whether freedom of speech and expression confined to Territory of India? - Held, constitution-makers have not chosen to limit extent of this freedom by adding words 'in territory of India' at end of art. 19(1)(a) - They have deliberately refrained from using any words of limitation - Further, merely because State action is restricted to territory of India, it does not necessarily follow that right of free speech and expression is also limited in its operation to territory of India and does not extend outside - Freedom of speech and expression guaranteed u/art. 19(1)(a) is exercisable not only inside country, but also outside - (B) Whether right to go abroad covered by art. 19(1)(a) or (g)? - Held, right to go abroad cannot be treated as part of the right to carry on trade, business, profession or calling guaranteed u/art. 19(1)(g) - Right to go abroad is clearly not a guaranteed right under any clause of art. 19(1) and s. 10(3)(c) which authorises imposition of restrictions on right to go abroad by impounding of passport cannot be held to be void as offending art. 19(1)(a) or (g), as its direct and inevitable impact is on right to go abroad and not on right of free speech and expression or right to carry on trade, business profession or calling - Further, though the right to go abroad is not included in the right contained in article 19(1)(a), if an order made u/s. 10(3)(c) of the Act does in fact violate the right of free speech and expression, such an order could be struck down as unconstitutional - (C) Whether order made u/s. 10(3)(c) may not violate Art. 19(1)(a) or (g)? - Held, expression 'interests of the general public' is a wide expression which covers within its broad sweep all kinds of interests of general public including interests of sovereignty and integrity of India, security of India and friendly relations of India with foreign States - Therefore, when an order is made u/s. 10(3)(c), which is in conformity with terms of that provision, it would be in interests of general public and even if it restricts freedom to carry on a profession, it would be protected by Art. 19(6) - If order cannot be shown to have been made in interests of public order, decency or morality, it would not only contravene Art. 19(1)(a), but would also be outside authority conferred by s. 10(3)(c) - Writ petition disposed of.

Constitution - Passport Act, 1967, s. 10(3)(c) - Constitution of India, 1950, arts. 14, 16 and 21 - Constitutionality of sub-section (3)(c) of s. 10 - Validity of order impounding passport - Petitioner's passport was impounded u/s. 10(3)(c) by Regional Passport Officer - Petitioner's request to furnish a copy of statement of reasons for making order as provided in s. 10(5) was refused on ground that Government decided 'in interest of general public' not to furnish her a copy of statement of reasons, for making of order - Present petition - (A) Petitioner submitted that the order is without jurisdiction and not 'in the interests of general public.' - Held, words 'in the interests of general public' are of a wide connotation but the authority in construing the facts of the case should determine whether in the interests of public the passport will have to be impounded - Whether the reasons given have any nexus to the interests of general public would depend upon the facts of each case - Plea that because of the vagueness of the words 'interests of the general public' in the order, the order itself is unsustainable, cannot be accepted - (B) Principles of natural justice - No opportunity of hearing was given - Held, an Authority u/s. 10(5) is bound to record in writing a brief statement of the reasons for making an order and furnish to the holder of the passport or travel document on demand a copy of the same, unless in any case, the passport authority is of the opinion that it will not be in the interests of the sovereignty and integrity of India, the security of India, friendly relation of India with any foreign country or in the interests of general public to furnish such a copy - However, case in which the authority declines to furnish reasons for making such an order would be extremely rare - In such cases it should be borne in mind that when the Government itself passes an order it should be presumed that it would have made the order after careful scrutiny - In the present case, there is no reason in declining to furnish to the petitioner the statement of reasons for impounding the passport but such a lapse by the authority would not make s. 10(5) ultra vires of the powers of the legislature - (C) Validity of Act - Held, validity of the Act will have to be examined on the basis whether it directly and immediately infringes on any of the fundamental rights of the petitioner - If a passport is refused according to procedure established by law, the plea that his other fundamental rights are denied cannot be raised if they are not directly infringed - (D) Whether Fundamental Rights, conferred under Part III and particularly the rights conferred u/art. 19 are available beyond the territory of India? - Held, Fundamental Rights are not absolute but are subject to reasonable restrictions provided for in the Constitution - Restrictions imposed are to be by operation of any existing law or making of a law by the Legislature imposing reasonable restrictions - Restrictions thus imposed, normally would apply only within the territory of India unless the legislation expressly or by necessary implication provides for extra-territorial operation - Neither in Art. 19 of the Constitution nor in any of the enactments restricting the rights u/art. 19(2) is there any provision expressly or by necessary implication providing for extra-territorial application - A citizen cannot enforce his Fundamental Rights outside the territory of India even if it is taken that such rights are available outside the country - In the view that a citizen is not entitled to the Fundamental Rights guaranteed u/art. 19 outside the territorial limits of India - In the present case, impugned order does not place any restrictions on the petitioner while she is away from India - Petitioner is not entitled to any of the fundamental rights enumerated in art. 19 of the Constitution and that the Passport Act complies with the requirements of Art. 21 of the Constitution and is in accordance with the procedure established by law - Further, s. 10(3)(c) to be construed as providing a right to the holder of the passport to be heard before the passport authority and that any order passed u/s. 10(3) is subject to a limited judicial scrutiny by the HC and SC - Writ Petition disposed of.
